B000N2HCM4|4.0|I found this book to be an interesting insight into our President's childhood and his personal self-discovery.<br /><br />Like many people who had an absent parent in their lives, he took the time to find out about the father he never really knew.  He visited his father's homeland, meet his half-siblings and relatives and learn that his father was not the successful man of the world that he thought he was, but that he was a dreamer who saw great things for himself but suffered the failures in life that many people do. And like many, he sought solace with alcohol.<br /><br />Barack also had to deal with his own racial identity because most of the world uses skin color to determine the social standing of each individual. Since there was no one in his home to serve as a role model to advise him in understanding how he fits into a such a world, he had to learn it himself by personal observation and interaction with both white and black people.<br /><br />Like anyone else, he had to grow up experiencing all of the thoughts, feelings and confusion that teens and young adults have, to become the person they are as an adult. We each choose a path that defines our life. Barack wanted to use his skills to help others help themselves.<br /><br />I don't understand all of the negative remarks by people that appear to have taken every sentence in this book as proof of some conspiracy to destroy America - it's absurd! One of the other reviews even uses quotes attributed to this book that don't actually appear in the book at all (fortunately, the Kindle version allows for searching the entire book). I believe that the people who write such reviews did so without actually reading the book. Such tactics drives me further away from that side of the political spectrum.<br /><br />And finally, I liked Barack's grandparents. They were real people - they had faults but were good people and great grandparents. I would have enjoyed having a beer Stanley and I'm sure that Barack misses him.<br /><br />I gave it 4 stars because it's not a literary masterpiece but a great insight to our 44th President, written well before his presidency.

B008BDZOZY|4.0|You have to hand it to Byrne for avoiding the mainstream yet continually coming up with memorable music for the past 35 years. He is undoubtedly a one-of-a-kind artist in the pop music world. I'd go so far as to say that he is the godfather of what is today known as Indie pop/rock. With each release, Byrne successful (more or less) challenges his listeners to embrace something new.<br /><br />With \\"Love This Giant\\" it is the addition of St. Vincent and the fact that each track is based around particular horn section arrangements--something that is unique in itself--I can't think of anyone else in the past 35 years that has attempted to make music such as this. That being said, it is still very much recognizable as something David Byrne would do. He always puts his undeniable, idiosyncratic stamp on whatever he does, thus making it totally David Byrne. St. Vincent, on the other hand, is less unique. I think she has trouble differentiating herself from Tori Amos, Fiona Apple, Regina Spektor, Adele, P!nk, Feist, Bjork, etc. There's so many Indie female singer/songwriters today, that St. Vincent sort of blends in.<br /><br />At first listen, \\"Love This Giant\\" didn't quite strike my liking, but after listening for two weeks straight, I find it quite enjoyable. Once I got my head around the horn arrangements and the lyrics and melody lines, everything started to hook itself into my mind. From first single (and excellent video) \\"Who\\", with its quintessential Byrne shouts and intellectual solipsisms about love, to \\"Ice Age\\" where St. Vincent latches on. To \\"I Am An Ape\\", \\"I Should Watch T.V.\\", \\"Optimist\\"--which for me might be my favorite track here.<br /><br />This isn't to say that \\"Love This Giant\\" hasn't any weak points. No, it does, especially at the end. \\"Lightning\\" might be the weakest track here along with \\"The One Who Broke Your Heart\\" and \\"Outside Space And Time\\". I deduct one star for this trio that closes the album on a somewhat weak note.<br /><br />So, is \\"Love This Giant\\" as good as 2008's Byrne/Eno collaboration, \\"Everything That Happens Will Happen Today\\"? No. Is it equal to, or better than, 2010's collaboration with Fatboy Slim, \\"Here Lies Love\\"? Yes. I'd say that \\"Love This Giant\\" is more focused, more succinct, and obviously, by default, much more brief and to the point than \\"Here Lies Love\\"--which certainly had its own merits to be sure.<br /><br />Also, let's take a moment to talk about collaborations. Don't you think St. Vincent is an odd choice, and a rather weak one? I mean, just think about who Byrne could have chosen. Sure, he's worked with Brian Eno, and that's a well established and logical collaboration. Working with Fatboy Slim was unusual, but also a rather weak choice. I mean, let's do some interesting pairings here such as Byrne/Tori Amos, now that would be interesting, or Byrne/Bjork, wouldn't that be something exciting? What about Byrne/Laurie Anderson? Two egghead intellectuals battling it out? How about Byrne/Peter Gabriel? Maybe these would all be obvious choices? How about Byrne/Trent Reznor? Byrne/Pitbull? Byrne/Shakira? The possibilities are endless. All I know is that when I saw he was working with St. Vincent I said to myself, who is that? I checked her music out, but I didn't think she was the right choice for Byrne. I mean, she's done just fine on \\"Love This Giant\\", but like Fatboy Slim, I felt Byrne could have done far better in choosing a collaborative partner.<br /><br />So here's how \\"Love This Giant\\" compares with Byrne's previous works:<br /><br />Byrne Solo:<br /><br />1981 My Life In The Bush Of Ghosts: Three Stars<br />1989 Rei Momo: Four Stars<br />1992 Uh-Oh: Four Stars<br />1994 David Byrne: Four Stars<br />1997 Feelings: Four Stars<br />2001 Look Into The Eyeball: Three and a Half Stars<br />2004 Grown Backwards: Three and a Half Stars<br />2008 Everything That Happens Will Happen Today: Four Stars<br />2010 Here Lies Love: Three and a Half Stars<br />2012 Love This Giant: Four Stars<br /><br />With Talking Heads:<br /><br />1977 Talking Heads '77: Five Stars<br />1978 More Songs About Buildings And Food: Five Stars<br />1979 Fear Of Music: Five Stars<br />1980 Remain In Light: Five Stars<br />1982 The Name Of This Band Is Talking Heads: Three Stars<br />1983 Speaking In Tongues: Four Stars<br />1984 Stop Making Sense: Five Stars<br />1985 Little Creatures: Four Stars<br />1986 True Stories: Four Stars<br />1988 Naked: Four Starsr Stars<br /><br />With Talking Heads:<br /><br />1977 Talking Heads '77: Five Stars<br />1978 More Songs About Buildings And Food: Five Stars<br />1979 Fear Of Music: Five Stars<br />1980 Remain In Light: Five Stars<br />1982 The Name Of This Band Is Talking Heads: Three Stars<br />1983 Speaking In Tongues: Four Stars<br />1984 Stop Making Sense: Five Stars<br />1985 Little Creatures: Four Stars<br />1986 True Stories: Four Stars<br />1988 Naked: Four Stars

B008RTE066|5.0|This Review is for the box set edition of  The Beatles Magical Mystery Tour 2012 release. I have used  the basic content in order as listed in the press release and on the box itself and will elaborate on it based on my viewing of  both the Blu Ray and DVD  set which I was lucky enough to receive on October 5th.<br /> The Beatles Magical Mystery Tour is basically their 1967 home movie which bewildered audiences at the time.<br />Today it allows us to look back at a time when the Summer of Love seemed endless and possibilities were limitless.<br />This box set contains the DVD & Blu-Ray, as well as a very nice softcover 60 page book with background information, photographs and production notes.The book also contains period reviews of the film,which were not so kind.and also includes a faithful reproduction of the mono double 7\\" vinyl EP of the films six  Beatles songs.The discs are pressed in the USA on reproduction Parlophone labels.,and mirror the original  British issue which accompanied the films 1967 TV premiere.The EP is remastered,possibly from the 2009 Mono Masters set,but I'm not sure.<br /><br />Special Features include:<br /><br />Director's Commentary - by Paul McCartney. Very anecdotal,with several interesting McCartneyesque quips,and some sharp barbs at BBC censors.<br /><br />The Making of Magical Mystery Tour (19m 05s) - Features interviews with Paul and Ringo, along with other cast members and crew. Includes unseen footage.Very enjoyable,and adds some much needed background to the proceedings<br /><br />Ringo the actor (2m 30s) - Ringo describes his role in the film and mentions his other acting experiences.<br /><br />Meet The Supporting Cast (11m 27s) -  Nat Jackley, Jessie Robins, Ivor Cutler, The Bonzo Dog Doo-Dah Band, Victor Spinetti, George Claydon, and Derek Royle are profiled.Again,not essential,but adds a little to the depth of the filmby way of additional outtake footage.<br /><br />`Your Mother Should Know' (2m 35s)<br />`Blue Jay Way' (3m 53s)<br />`The Fool On The Hill' (3m 05s)<br />- These three songs are presented in previously unseen  edited editions.  These alternate versions will especially be interesting to Beatle collectors,as they have not been released officially or bootlegged.They are,however,not quite as crisp visually as the remastered film itself.<br /><br />`Hello Goodbye' (3m 24s), as featured in Top of the Pops 1967<br />- The Beatles had the BBC  film them in the editing process  when they were working on Magical Mystery Tour. the resulting film was edited  to create a promo which was subsequently  broadcast on Top of the Pops.  Done in Black and white,this is a great addition to any Beatles collection and it also makes its DVD debut here.<br /><br />Nat's Dream (2m 50s) - This deleted scene from the original film was directed by John Lennon  and features Nat Jackley.<br /> Of interest to Beatle collectors is the music soundtrack to this deleted scene,\\"Shirley's Wild Accordion\\",a Lennon-McCartney instrumental composition,recorded at Abbey Road during the Blue Jay Way sessions on October 12,1967. and produced By John Lennon. This features Shirley Evans on accordion,Paul McCartney on maraca,with some incidental background yelling,and Ringo Starr on drums.Never released until now.<br /><br />Ivor Cutler - `I'm Going In A Field' (2m 35s) - Cutler  is featured performing on a keyboard `I'm Going In A Field',  and it is the weakest of all the bonus material,.This deleted scene features no other cast members.<br /><br />Traffic - `Here We Go Round The Mulberry Bush' (1m 53s) -Traffic's excellent  `Here We Go Round The Mulberry Bush',is slightly reminiscent of the Strawberry fields forever Beatles promo. It was commissioned by The Beatles who at one point planned to include it in the film.It survives today as an outtake.<br /><br />All in all this box set is a beautifully packaged  commemorative edition,with the book containing a diary of events as they unfolded.<br />The DVDs are in a laminated  folder and individually enclosed in laminated mini sleeves for protection.<br />The vinyl  mono EP is also a faithful reproduction,but the sleeves seem a little too snug for the enclosed discs.The remastered film is beautifully reproduced ,both  visually and musically.I will have listen more closely to note sonic differences between the Blue Ray and the regular DVD.At present,to my ear The Blue Ray  5.1 stereo is about as good as it gets.<br />As far as the film itself,It should be looked at for what it is,53 minutes of the Beatles being themselves.That should be good enough for anyone.<br />As far as the price,I hedged a bit at first,but such a quality package makes it an essential purchase for the avid Beatle collector.

B005HJU7HA|5.0|Sherlock Holmes is popular on both the large and small screens.  But he is portrayed in wildly divergent ways.  This is why reading the original makes sense for fans.  Don't worry; these stories are not as long and boring as you would find in 'fine literature'.  They were  written for popular consumption and apparently the regular people back then preferred fast-moving stories just as much as we do now.<br /><br />The kindle version of this book is perfectly formatted, no problems there, and is quite lengthy, so you can get many hours of entertainment at a low cost per page.  Don't waste your money on a new best-seller.  Buy this instead.<br /><br />Reading the original allows you to compare the screen stories with what Doyle actually wrote and draw your own judgements about whether modern versions have stretched a point too far with their creative updates.  Is Lucy Lui (CBS) a fair replica of Watson?  Is the latest PBS version of the Holmes-Watson relationship off-base with its homosexual implications?  Was Holmes a drug addict?  Did he have Asperger's syndrome? Was he a good boxer?<br /><br />The stories also raise questions that are more difficult to answer. What the heck is brain fever? Why does Watson describe Holmes as 'bohemian'?  Was renting a flat typical for professionals in the Victorian Age?  Would Doyle have agreed with Dickens about how bad the era really was?  Reading Doyle makes me want to live in that time and place.<br /><br />Your enjoyment of Holmes on screen and in the theater will be enhanced if you read this book, whether it is for the first time or whether it is a revisit to a bygone era.

B000FBFN1U|2.0|I am an avid reader of genre fiction, literature, criticism and sometimes abstract non-fiction, and was pre-disposed to enjoy George RR. Martin's Storm of Swords, having become enamored of HBO's production Game of Thrones. But I find the prose very slow going. Martin excels at plot, and nearly every sentence in the opening 50 pages, at least, suggests multiple plot lines, so many that as a reader I find iti difficult to a) keep track of them all and b) determine which ones are significant, which are throw-aways. The density does not seem to let up in order to let my mind breathe and take in what's just been said. Every paragraph is a thicket.<br /><br />I have not read the previous books, but due to the tv show I recognize the characters. Characterization may be Martin's weak point as a writer; he can draw a distinctive character quickly, but they don't yet feel to me as if they acquire much substance, except through their actions, which it seems to me the author is pushing forward like a computer games player moving avatars around. Maybe if I get further in this book I will feel that the individuals who make up this multitude do indeed \\"live\\" on the page. The density of incident and anticipated incident, however, is wearing me down. The density does not equal intensity -- I feel little drive here, though the multiple plots offer plenty of intrigue. Unlike in the tv show, where the cross editing between subplots is relentless but makes for suspense, I am not on edge here, or inspired to keep reading. That is a failing in any book, and I'm disappointed that it's my reaction to this one.

B000002ITM|4.0|Alex Skolnick's last album until \\"The Formation of Damnation\\" and undeniably his best performance with this band. His ethereal, monumental building solo in Return To Serenity still stuns me after 100 listens, and is unquestionably one of the finest solos in the genre. Also his smooth bluesy lines in \\"Electric Crown\\" and eerie jaw-dropping work in So Many Lies and the title track (the latter which includes seriously awesome jazz phrasing) is stunning. I've heard him in recent albums but unfortunately the man seems stuck in autopilot shred mode and doesn't utilize his more interesting influences (he did after all attend Guiliard, play free form jazz, and plays in the Trans Siberian Orchestra). This is the last album he made before leaving Testament, probably feeling they limited his style too much. And his ability to push the limits of metal guitar work to extreme technical and creative limits is shown at his finest here. In fact, I'd argue he's second only Marty Friedman of Megadeth fame (another man who left his mark on metal by using a wide variety of influences in his guitar work).<br /><br />Most of the songs here are good; the overall songwriting is the best the band had since \\"The New Order\\". To all the haters, despite clearly not being thrash, this record is much less commercial than the black album. It also contains some of the bands most creative songs, like the morbid, creepy title track , and the awesome ballad Return to Serenity. Electric Crown the lead off single is one of the band's catchiest songs ever, and I've always like the crunchy song \\"Deadline\\" which has a very catchy chorus and two excellent winding solos from Mr. Skolnick. 0060899220|4.0|\\"Kitchen Confidential\\" is less a memoir, and more a collection of Anthony Bourdain's essays, thoughts, and meditations on the restaurant kitchen, and what it means for those who work in it and for those who are fed by it.  The first few pieces in this 307-page book are hilarious to read, and perhaps some stories -- especially the one about how when as a lowly busboy/runner Bourdain peeked at the chef banging a bride on her wedding day it was his divine inspiration to become a chef -- are too good to be true, and seem if not fabricated then exaggerated.<br /><br />There's a section where Bourdain provides useful advice for restaurant eaters.  Never order fish on a Monday, and never order a steak well-done.  Don't try the buffets or the specials because they're just recycled food.  Actually, what's best is if you don't eat at a restaurant at all because you never know who has touched your food, and where they have put your food  (the thought of criminal, decrepit, and slightly insane chefs banging waitresses on bags of flour will make you think twice about eating pizza and bread at a restaurant again).<br /><br />There are some entertaining moments in this book, and there are some useful advice.  But really it's too much information, and it seems all haphazardly assembled together so that this book feels like a 1,000 pages rather than 307 pages.  Bourdain of all people should know that a good book should be like a good meal.  Yes, you want intense flavor, brilliant colors, and rich variety in the meal, but there ought to be a consistency and compatibility from the appetizer to the desert.  A good meal is ultimately a good story, and you permit flavors, ideas, and plot-lines to build as you entice the reader throughout.<br /><br />As it stands, this book is less the work of a chef at a two-star Michelin restaurant, and more like that of an all-you-can-eat buffet place.  There are some real gems in here, but there's also a lot of gratuitous chapters put in just so Bourdain can deliver a manuscript without much effort and the publisher can sell books.

I tried turning on the LS-10's \\"low cut\\" filter.  This cut out the noise, but also makes voice recording sound too thin.  I think I'll just deal with the low-frequency noise in post. I set the LS-10's \\"mic sense\\" setting to high, and turned down the recording level gain, and this seemed to be a better way to configure things.<br /><br />In short, the purchase looks like a good one. B008CJ0JTI|5.0|David Fincher has always had a loyal fan base. His films have been winning people over for twenty years with films like \\"Fight Club\\" and \\"Se7en\\" developing a cult-like following. Criterion Collection's release of \\"The Game\\" feels like some lucky schmuck discovered diamonds in an abandoned coal mine. Now remastered with loads of special features, \\"The Game\\" continues Fincher's near flawless streak of impeccable filmmaking.<br /><br />Films that are capable of making you think and turning your brain inside out are personal favorites; \\"Inception,\\" \\"Oldboy,\\" \\"The Machinist,\\" and \\"Fight Club\\" are spectacular examples. \\"The Game\\" can be categorized right along with the best of them. Nicholas Van Orton (Michael Douglas) is a successful investment banker complete with Swiss bank accounts, fancy cars, and a huge house; Nicholas has it all. But he was noticeably unhappy and the death of his father from when he was a boy still haunts him.<br /><br />On his 48th birthday, his ex-wife Elizabeth (Anna Katarina) along with all of his co-workers and his maid Ilsa (Carroll Baker) wish him a happy birthday but Nicholas views it as just another day. That is until his brother Conrad (Sean Penn) introduces him to CRS - Consumer Recreational Services. Shrouded in secrecy, Nicholas has no idea what to expect. Little does he realize that his life is about to be on the line along with his fortune.<br /><br />The choreography in this psychological thriller is brilliant. It helps establish the rich atmosphere of the film whether it's the fluorescent hum of office building lighting, the slow rotating shots as Nicholas eats his birthday dinner in his home, or the incredible break-in sequence filled with graffiti that's infused by black lighting. Just like all of Fincher's work, it pulls you in so easily. The story is constantly engaging. It's nearly impossible to predict how far this game is going to go and at what lengths Nicholas will go to discover the truth.<br /><br />The performances are fantastically solid. Michael Douglas does an excellent job of portraying a man who once had everything and basically has nothing by the end of the film. While Sean Penn has a small amount of screen time, his performance is different every time he shows up; calm and collected, frantic and borderline insane, and eventually the understanding brother. It's also worth paying particular attention to Deborah Kara Unger because it's so difficult to determine just whose side the Christine character is on.<br /><br />Well-written, expertly paced, and undeniably riveting, \\"The Game\\" is perhaps most impressive in the way it strips down the Nicholas Van Orton character. The whole process is very layered and each layer breaks down Nicholas even more than the last. Never seeing the film beforehand certainly helped, but Criterion Collection's release of \\"The Game\\" is definitely a must own.<br /><br />The Criterion Collection director-approved special edition Blu-ray includes a new, restored digital transfer, supervised by director of photography Harris Savides with original theatrical 5.1 surround soundtrack and presented in DTS-HD Master Audio, Alternate 5.1 surround mix optimized for home theater viewing, supervised by sound designer Ren Klyce and presented in DTS-HD Master Audio, Audio Commentary by director David Fincher, actor Michael Douglas, screenwriters John Brancato and Michael Farris, director of Photography Harris Savides, production designer Jeffrey Beecroft, and visual effets supervisor Kevin Haug, an Alternate Ending, an hour's worth of behind the scenes footage and film-to storyboard comparisons for four of the film's major set pieces (Dog Chase, The Taxi, Christine's House, The Fall), the Psychological Test Film in its entirety, the teaser and trailer of the film with optional commentary, and a booklet featuring an essay by film critic David Sterritt.fantastically solid. Michael Douglas does an excellent job of portraying a man who once had everything and basically has nothing by the end of the film. While Sean Penn has a small amount of screen time, his performance is different every time he shows up; calm and collected, frantic and borderline insane, and eventually the understanding brother. It's also worth paying particular attention to Deborah Kara Unger because it's so difficult to determine just whose side the Christine character is on.<br /><br />Well-written, expertly paced, and undeniably riveting, \\"The Game\\" is perhaps most impressive in the way it strips down the Nicholas Van Orton character. The whole process is very layered and each layer breaks down Nicholas even more than the last. Never seeing the film beforehand certainly helped, but Criterion Collection's release of \\"The Game\\" is definitely a must own.<br /><br />The Criterion Collection director-approved special edition Blu-ray includes a new, restored digital transfer, supervised by director of photography Harris Savides with original theatrical 5.1 surround soundtrack and presented in DTS-HD Master Audio, Alternate 5.1 surround mix optimized for home theater viewing, supervised by sound designer Ren Klyce and presented in DTS-HD Master Audio, Audio Commentary by director David Fincher, actor Michael Douglas, screenwriters John Brancato and Michael Farris, director of Photography Harris Savides, production designer Jeffrey Beecroft, and visual effets supervisor Kevin Haug, an Alternate Ending, an hour's worth of behind the scenes footage and film-to storyboard comparisons for four of the film's major set pieces (Dog Chase, The Taxi, Christine's House, The Fall), the Psychological Test Film in its entirety, the teaser and trailer of the film with optional commentary, and a booklet featuring an essay by film critic David Sterritt.

B007TK77QE|4.0|As a longtime Rowling fan, I was intrigued by the synopsis of Casual Vacancy, and curious to experience Rowling's mastery in an entirely new genre.  But I think I had fewer expectations than some other readers that Rowling's adult work would be \\"nice\\".  It isn't.  And that's ok.  A writer whose work for children was accused of being \\"too dark\\" and \\"too scary\\" isn't going to write something sweetly pretty for adults.<br /><br />With such different topic matter, it would be unfair to draw comparisons, yet it is almost inevitable that we will.  Like the HP books, there is humour here... but it's sardonic humour.  Like the HP books, there is a willingness to describe the unpleasantness of people... but less focus on good conquering evil.  And like the HP books, there is death here too... but not the glorious deaths that occur in a battle against evil; in Casual Vacancy the deaths are hopeless, helpless, and despairing.  They leave unfinished business, and messy, unsolvable problems.  But that, surely, is the whole point; that life is like that, people are like that, and it behoves us to make the best we can of what we have, both for ourselves and for others.<br /><br />Perhaps the worst that can be said about Casual Vacancy is that it shows traces of the fact that Rowling didn't have to write it - it doesn't appear quite as finely honed as the HP books, and at times (particularly in the first half of the book) it lacks their compulsive pace.  I found myself much more aware of Rowling's skill as a writer in Casual Vacancy, and much less engaged in the story - perhaps because there really isn't a huge amount of story.  Lots of character insights and introspection, but not a complex or sustained plot line, which unfortunately leaves the skill a little too obvious when it ought to be concealed.  The last 20% of it is very impressive, though, and lifted it from 3 stars to 3.5 in my view.  I gave it four in the end because I felt there were so many unfairly negative reviews that I decided to over-estimate rather than under-estimate.<br /><br />But all in all, Rowling has written a raw portrayal of the underbelly of civilisation, and the conflict between petty meanness and courage.  She, at least, has not averted her eyes.  Barry Fairbrother would be proud of her.hat I decided to over-estimate rather than under-estimate.<br /><br />But all in all, Rowling has written a raw portrayal of the underbelly of civilisation, and the conflict between petty meanness and courage. 0767802470|5.0|I first saw this movie in a theater. I was a serving Navy officer. I knew a little about its history. I was on the edge of my seat beginning to end. I remember sweating in a fully air-conditioned movie house. I do not remember reading the captions but I do not know German. This movie is that powerful.<br /><br />The background of this movie is important to understand. This movie is a very cut down version of a 6 hour mini series made for the German television. It is a fictionalized account of events as experienced by its author: novelist Lothar-Gunther Buchheim.<br />He had served as a writer for the WWII German Army Signal magazine. As such he rode in a German U Boat and originaly wrote a propaganda version of his experience.<br /><br />His later book and the mini series were highly controversial in Germany. Some thought it honored the Nazis and should have been illegal under German law.<br /><br />My points:<br />1.The back story to this movie is important.<br />2.It is based on first person experience - now absent the propaganda.<br />3.The movie is about 1/3 of what the writer and director originally produced and broadcast.<br /><br />Plot summery:<br /> World War II has reached a turning point for the German U Boat Navy. Each new mission is undertaken at a higher risk and with less chance to score hits. Crews are beginning to feel the strain. An army war correspondent is assigned to go on patrol with one of the more successful u-boats. His assignment is to write about his experience.<br />Historical note: The loss rate among German Submariners in WWII was so high that sailors referred to U-Boats as Iron Coffins<br />We share his experience. Like him we cannot know or understand everything we see, but we cannot help but understand what the experience feels like.<br /><br />On this mission, all does not go well.<br /><br />This is not a pro Nazi movie. It is neither pro nor anti-war. Fundamentally it is not a movie about submarines.<br /><br />This is what happens to any dedicated, decent human when you send him to war.<br />At some level we understand that war means that some people will be killed or physically injured. We are beginning to understand that some will experience psychological harm. This movie is about the boredom, the terror and the sum total personal weight of being in combat.<br /><br />It is left to you to decide if and when war is necessary. This movie asks that you understand what you are doing to people when you make that decision.<br /><br />It is worth noting that this crew is not politically motivated. They are not interested in the Nazis. In fact the one officer who is most deeply committed to the Nazi cause is a common object of derision. In the book, this officer suffers a case of the crabs so severe as to have them crawling visibly in his eyebrows.<br />I suspect that this is something of a white-wash of the non SS portion of the German military. My experience of folks in uniform is that they tend to be politically to the right. German antisemitism, anti-Slavic feelings and other extreme attitudes exploited by the Nazis were endemic in the total German population of that period. The movie however has no praise for any politics. Politics is not what this movie is about.<br /><br />Other Reviews expressed technical issues with this print. I did not have any technical problems.<br />Other reviews were not happy with the dubbing or the captions. I find this movie so involving that the translations/dubbing become subordinate to the feel, the experience and the emotions of this movie. Perhaps if I understood German I might feel slighted by the text.<br /><br />At some point this move should grab you. At that point it is about the totality of the experience and words tend to carry less meaning.<br /><br />This is not just a great movie, it is an important movies to any dedicated, decent human when you send him to war.<br />At some level we understand that war means that some people will be killed or physically injured. 1400052181|4.0|What can I say that hasn't been said already?  Yes, I was fascinated to learn about the history of cell research.  Yes, I found the moral, ethical, and psychological implications of cell research compelling.  But I think what really stood out for me - the thing I'll remember most about the book - is how it gave voice to a class of Americans that are shockingly unrepresented in literature.  Deborah and the other relatives of  Henrietta Lacks are members of a largely \\"forgotten\\" class here in the U.S. - tragically under/uneducated, superstitious, plagued with physical and mental illness, trapped in a cycle of dependency on social safety nets that provide for basic needs without providing resources to help people better their condition.  It may be tempting to be frustrated by Deborah's irrational thinking, her superstition, her ignorance, her wild swings of mood - but her love for her family, her compassion for others, and her humanity remind us that she is \\"us\\" - or the \\"us\\" most of us would be if we'd been subject to the same psychological and socioeconomic upbringing as she and her family members.<br /><br />For me, the book is less about the ethics of cell research than the ethics of throwing \\"services\\" - health care, food stamps, medicare - at our forgotten class and considering our job as a society \\"done\\".  Until we ensure that citizens of the U.S. receive an adequate education, I'm not sure we can ever achieve true \\"informed consent.\\"<br /><br />Whoops!  Didn't mean to preach - just meant to say that, in my opinion, the popularity of the book is probably largely due to the fact that it works on so many levels.  Unlike Poisoner's Bible, Disappearing Spoon, or Devil in the White City - other non-fiction works that have been built around stories of scientific progress - this book has real humans, and real humanity, at its heart.  Long after I've forgotten everything I just learned about Hayfield Limits and telomeres, I'll remember Deborah and her struggle to be treated with dignity and humanity by a society that owes her mother so much.

0375842209|4.0|It's going to be challenging to write this review because I'm not sure through which \\"lens\\" through which to describe it.  As a life-long reader with a penchant for challenging fiction, should I review Book Thief as a work of adult literature; or, in my role as a middle school English teacher, should I address its merits as a tool for teaching literature to teens?  As it happens, this book wholly succeeds on both levels.<br /><br />The key is that Book Thief possesses much more depth than usually found in literature marketed to teens. The characters are complex and deeply realized.  The text is stuffed with imagery, personification, extended metaphors/allegories (Max's stories), irony (the use of Mein Kampf in aiding Max's escape), and symbolism (those books!!).  Finally, the author's voice is vivid, lyric, and utterly compelling.  Speaking of which ...<br /><br />Much has been made of the author's choice of Death as the narrator.  I gather from an interview with the author that I recently read, this decision was largely fortuitous; certainly it was fortunate!  Any story about Nazi Germany can't end well, but by personifying Death, making him (it?) almost compassionate, the deaths become a little less horrific.  A group of Jewish prisoners leaping to their death from a precipice is ghastly - but somehow the idea that Death is there, deliberately catching their souls before they land, makes it more bearable.  Zusak's use of foreshadowing is similarly cunning: by planting clues in section/chapter titles and in Death's off-hand remarks about shocks/horrors to come, the author provides readers a chance to emotionally prepare for them, so that they are (almost) endurable.  I contrast this with works like Schindlers List, which are so unrelentingly horrific that they are almost impossible to read.  In contrast, Zusak has found a way to present horrific events without compelling us to run away from them.<br /><br />Another benefit is that this device allows the author to utilize a narrative voice entirely unbound by preconceived notions.  Because, face it, is there any period of history about which Americans possess more preconceived notions than Nazi Germany?  But by utilizing a narrator whose perspective is entirely bereft of emotion (not to mention clich), the author allows us to experience even the most familiar elements of his tale - from ideas as innocuous as weather to themes as vast as morality - in ways that fresh, stripped of subliminal baggage, and provocative.<br /><br />Thanks to all of the above, Book Thief accomplishes what few books about Nazi Germany accomplish, spinning a tale that challenges stereotypes; that presents rather than judges; that depicts humiliation without stripping its characters of dignity; and that forces us to abandon our pre-packaged \\"mental newsreels\\" of Nazi Germany in order to replace the images with real faces, real lives, real emotions, real epiphanies, and real understanding.

B004LROUW2|3.0|First, let me say that this was my first Murakami novel. A number of people have said that 1Q84 is not the right book to start with, if you want to get into this author, and perhaps I should have listened. Murakami has a distinct style. His greatest strength perhaps is his ability to create a mood that is just a bit off, so that the entire time you're reading this book you feel the world is not quite right--almost a Kafkaesque kind of feeling.<br /><br />His characters are fascinating, quirky but distant. I'm tempted to say they are flat, but on closer inspection, they're complex with pained backstories, their distance more the result of alienation than weak writing. The two protagonists, Tengo and Aomame fit this description. They wander through their early adulthood with a sense of isolation bordering on despair, both independently clinging to one shining moment in their childhood when they touched each other. But in a world of alienation, they are unable to meet. So they enter an alternate reality, a world slightly off, where Japanese police carry guns and two moons rise in the night sky, at least for some people. Murakami has accomplished what all great writers do, pulling me into his world and connecting me to strong characters.<br /><br />So why three stars. This author knows how to write a plot. In places the story races along, making the reader turn pages and want to know more. But he seems to feel no obligation to be consistent or finish what he starts. Sure, Tengo and Aomame do finally get together (not a spoiler--that should be obvious to any reader from the start). But he introduces major new characters after several hundred pages (The investigator, Ushikawa, appears late and dominates a portion of the last third of the book, a point-of-view character, needed to further the plot, but overdeveloped). I believe Murakami's greatest sin is to introduce us to his most intriguing character, the little girl Fuka-Eri and then drop her like a hot potato. Fuka-Eri is author of the mysterious story Air Chrysalis, fugitive from the mysterious Sakigake cult and conduit to the Little People, all major plot elements that keep you reading for hundreds of pages. And then suddenly--never mind. She was part of the alternate world and just goes away.<br /><br />Over nine hundred pages is a lot of book to create a mood and place intriguing characters in a strange alternate world. The book is well written and fast paced for most of the story. But this author deemed it unnecessary to tie major plot elements together at the end, leaving me feeling cheated.is author of the mysterious story Air Chrysalis, fugitive from the mysterious Sakigake cult and conduit to the Little People, all major plot elements that keep you reading for hundreds of pages. B0071XO8RA|5.0|What else can I say that others have not said? All these 5-star ratings are real. Intrigued by the overwhelming positive reviews given on this book, I joined the bandwagon and experienced for myself the thrill of learning how it is to live in a Silo. I was a little unconvinced during the first 2 chapters as the author disappointingly killed one main character to another- the sheriff, the mayor, the deputy sheriff- and left me wondering what's on the author's mind. When another character, Juliette, was sent to cleaning, I thought the story will evolve with another one- Lukas, maybe - but much to my surprise, it did not end the way I thought it would be. The author even managed to introduce new characters by the end of the book, giving more meaning and justice to the plot.  Hence, the book deserves no less than five (5) stars for unpredictability.<br /><br />The setting of the story was also a fresh idea. The author effectively created a different kind of world with its own law and culture. I liked how the structure of the Silo defined the people's way of life and affected its political system, peace and order, economic distribution, and modes of communication. The characters are, unquestionably, humans as demonstrated by very familiar emotions, such as physical strain, kinship, love, and desire for power. But how these people lived and survived a constrained place, such as the Silo, is something to look forward to. The author built a place where I simply do not see myself living in. In this case, the book deserves another five (5) stars for uniqueness and creativity.<br /><br />And while the characters were as enthralling as the Silo(s), the book will definitely knock you off your feet and leave you wanting for more. In passing, this book reminds me of another book I recently read entitled, The Lodestone Trilogy ([[ASIN:B006OOC5MC The Lodestone Trilogy (Limited Edition) (The Lodestone Series)]]). I was never a fan of sci-fi or epic novels, but these are books I highly recommend to readers like me.  Although the finale was fantastic as it is, I still hope that the author will publish a sequel to the book relaying how Mayor Juliette will run Silo 18, how Solo and the kids will start a new life in Silo 17 (and how Juliette will contribute in rebuilding it), and to completely describe what is going on in Silo 20!  With the discovery of heat tapes, I can also imagine how people can start traveling from one Silo to another. Two thumbs up for a great story, and another five (5) stars to the author for his impressive flair in writing.

B0083DJUMA|5.0|I feel that many reviewers will \\"review\\" the technologies referenced in this book, rather than the book itself. So I would like to first make a comment on the technology, then I will speak about the book.<br /><br />I am constantly less than satisfied with products that I buy because of poor build quality, unnecessary complexity, lacking functionality, or inability to personalize features; one simple example, for instance, might be TV remote controls - the battery cover gets lost, the buttons are hard to find in the dark and half the controller includes DVD functionality (Pause, Fast Forward, etc.) when all I am typically trying to do is turn on the TV and PS3 for some Netflix. I digress, but you can tell, this is a peeve of mine. When I read this book, it gives me hope that I might be able to deal with such common frustrations. The naysayers will trivialize these tools, lamenting that they fall short of nanotechnological matter compilers, but then, well, they are missing the point. No tool is best for all situations, but new tools should always be welcome.<br /><br />Before you let my above comment make you believe that I am just a materialistic American who expects New Things to solve my problems (I am, but I do it with self-awareness) I would also like to say that I also believe that these technologies will have a much more profound impact on developing nations and cultures. Designing ultra-low-cost products not just for but with and alongside the ultra-poor (<$1/day) will become more and more possible with these types of manufacturing technologies. I fully expect to see CAD (Cardboard Aided Design) and Maker Labs in use in Africa in the near future. Local design solutions and local manufacturing - a formative duo.<br /><br />On the book itself I appreciate that it is rather reasonably sized and not so full of repetitive content that I started skimming to get to the point; I finished the book in just a few days, and it was jam packed with information. His book isfirst and foremost a book about people and secondly about the technology. If you are interested in 3D Printing (the poster child of this movement), CNC robotics (Computer Numerical Control), 3D scanners, crowdsourcing, Arduino microcontrollers, and the culture of tinkering, this book will offer a clear and concise vision of that future. If you are already fully informed about all these technologies, this book will offer case studies in those who have already started to see success in this area, and some of the methods on how they did it.<br /><br />For all those out there who are struggling in our fledgeling economy, working dead-end jobs without even a cost of living raise, perhaps this book will incite you to take the situation into your own hands. Certainly there is opportunity here, and this book can help point you in many promising directions. Highly recommended.

B0019T9FL6|4.0|Jennifer Kate Hudson astounded the world with her outstanding performance alongside the likes of Beyonce' and Eddie Murphy in the movie adaption of the Broadway classic [[ASIN:B000O1799U Dreamgirls]]. This was a demanding role,especially from a relative newcomer. And Hudson not only made an enormous impression vocally in the film,but pulled off her role from an acting perspective so convincingly that she is one of the few personalities of that era who almost upstaged Beyonce',even though it was by no means intentional. It was clear that Hudson was going to have a major impact as a recording artist. The soul/R&B world she was entering into was filled with negligible vocal talents backed up by fair to middling music,to be kind. Especially when it came to female performers. They often were more in the position of being recording super models,more concerned with fashion and makeup than musical talent. So hooking up with Clive Davis at Arista,Hudson basically came at the music world from the opposite end of the spectrum.<br /><br />  \\"Spotlight\\" begins the album with one of it's very best numbers,a vibrant late 70's/early 80's post disco dance/funk number that introduces Hudson's booming soul belt of a voice with a melodically strong number very much worthy of the vocalists abilities. The slower material such as \\"If This Isn't Love\\",\\"Giving Myself\\",\\"You Pulled Me Through\\",\\"Invisible\\" and the dynamic \\"Can't Stop The Rain\\" get the full on Aretha treatment where her vocal relationship with the music and melody is so close knit it seems a sure fire chance she wrote the songs herself. For a track featuring a cliched guest rapper the beat boxing and harmonically rich \\"Pocketbook\\",with Ludacris not only has a spicy sense of humor about it but is extremely funky musically and his rap goes well with the song. Cannot say the same for \\"What's Wrong (Go Away) with T-Pain,which is too defined by it's cliches of autotune and too much attention focused on the guest where..well one might feel they want to take the song title literally.<br /><br />  Hudson brings out the heavy southern soul flavor on both \\"I'm His Only Woman\\",a sassy two timer anthem with her and Fantasia as \\"the two lovers\\" and \\"And I'm Telling You I'm Not Going\\". Musically and vocally,this challenging gospel style soul number is the highlight of the entire album with Hudson and the band bursting out of the music with so much emotional expression that Hudson seems like she will hold her final note in the song for an eternity. The closer is the more obvious gospel of \\"Jesus Promised Me A Home Over There\\",done more subtly. In the end,this is not a perfect album. But few of the best debuts are. It often isn't fair to the rest of an artists career if it is. But the majority of the album is extremely strong,and musically varied just to the right point where a vocalist of Hudson's caliber is able to discover what levels of soul and grooves she's best at dealing with. 0547569653|5.0|Eat & Run is the autobiography of the greatest ultramarathoner of our time. In 17 years, Scott Jurek ran 100 ultramarathons, an average of one every two months, winning 34 of them, placing second in 16 of those races and setting 12 new course records. But to Scott, running is much more than simply winning and setting records. Running is about reaching toward perfection, about stopping time and about running through and beyond all physical and mental limits. Scott runs to lose himself, to connect with something larger and to experience the beauty and timelessness of the present moment in sharp focus.<br /><br />In this book, Scott brings the reader along with him on 21 of his runs, freely sharing tips on his methods of preparation, clothing, diet, psychological stages, competitive strategy and philosophy of success. The reader will learn how and why he became a complete vegan, his fascinating relationships with motivators, pacers and competitors, and how he used his painful upbringing to become the most successful at his craft. There are also many references to other top running books, his favorite recipes, the sciences of nutrition and fitness and his four step checklist for assessing in-race injuries.<br /><br />My favorite paragraph was Scott listing the five ways of reaching satori, the sudden Zen-like clarity known in English as \\"the zone\\". He says he has experienced the zone not only when past the point of maximum effort, but also through prayer, meditation, on a five mile jog and mindfully chopping a carrot. That's good news for those of us who long for runner's high, but who will never run even one marathon, let alone run for 24 continuous hours.<br /><br />I recommend Eat & Run for serious runners, for less serious joggers and for anyone curious about the physical, psychological and spiritual aspects of eating and, of course, running.
